  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_43801add-0d2c-487d-913a-480c67e47db2'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer17182523349674369259">"I have noticed that sometimes I frighten people; what they really fear is themselves. They think it is I who scare them, but it is the dwarf within them, the ape-faced manlike being who sticks up his head from the depths of their souls."<br/><br/>PÃ¤r Lagerkvist's richly philosophical novel <i>The Dwarf</i> is an exploration of individual and social identity. The novel, set in a time when </span>
  <span id="freeText17182523349674369259" style="display:none">"I have noticed that sometimes I frighten people; what they really fear is themselves. They think it is I who scare them, but it is the dwarf within them, the ape-faced manlike being who sticks up his head from the depths of their souls."<br /><br />PÃ¤r Lagerkvist's richly philosophical novel <i>The Dwarf</i> is an exploration of individual and social identity. The novel, set in a time when Italian towns feuded over the outcome of the last feud, centers on a social outcast, the court dwarf Piccoline. From his special vantage point Piccoline comments on the court's prurience and on political intrigue as the town is gripped by a siege. Gradually, Piccoline is drawn deeper and deeper into the conflict, and he inspires fear and hate around him as he grows to represent the fascination of the masses with violence.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_976560264" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/976560264" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/976560264?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=unforgettable">unforgettable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=nobels">nobels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=so-good-it-hurts">so-good-it-hurts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer976560264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3955297714023584363">"Look on my works ye mighty and despair!" <br/><br/>Anno Diaboli 1944, PÃ¤r Lagerkvist wrote a tale of destruction and pain, a study in hatred and hubris.<br/><br/>Set at a splendid, powerful court in Renaissance Italy, it shows human culture and civilisation in its most exquisite form. Led by a charismatic and strong prince, the courtiers live a life filled with festivities, art, science, fashion and sexual adventures. The court artist is a thinly veiled portrait of Renaissance genius Leonardo da Vinci.<br/><br/>Underneath t</span>
  <span id="freeText3955297714023584363" style="display:none">"Look on my works ye mighty and despair!" <br /><br />Anno Diaboli 1944, PÃ¤r Lagerkvist wrote a tale of destruction and pain, a study in hatred and hubris.<br /><br />Set at a splendid, powerful court in Renaissance Italy, it shows human culture and civilisation in its most exquisite form. Led by a charismatic and strong prince, the courtiers live a life filled with festivities, art, science, fashion and sexual adventures. The court artist is a thinly veiled portrait of Renaissance genius Leonardo da Vinci.<br /><br />Underneath the liberal, civilised surface however, evil reigns. The hateful narrator, a court dwarf, tells the story of the community from the perspective of the badly treated, violently ridiculed and bullied outsider. Never part of the homogeneous and privileged caste of courtiers, the dwarf secretly nurtures his hatred towards human happiness and pleasure, especially in the form of physical love, which is denied him due to his different body. His feeling of humiliation and inferiority fuels his hatred for humanity, and he begins to spin a web of intrigue that leads to disaster.<br /><br />Slowly but steadily, life in the city dissolves into chaos. A disastrous military adventure ends in defeat, and an attempt to take back control through a treacherous and murderous trick ends in a siege of the city which causes famine, starvation, and ultimately plague and death. The inner circle of the court is similarly affected by the destruction, and the dwarf uses every opportunity to stifle any feeling of love and compassion, and to instill hatred and jealousy in the family he serves. He leaves a trail of death and despair.<br /><br />As suffering replaces pleasure, the dwarf's clever abuse of religious doctrine leads to fanatic belief and self-castigation. Is the plague a sign of divine punishment? Is the death of a lover caused by sinful sexual liberality? Insanity, grief and mourning gain power over all protagonists, and they stumble ever deeper into the maelstrom of violent self-destruction, until they face the ruins of their once beautiful court.<br /><br />The dwarf, confiding to his journal that he is applauding each step deeper into the abyss and celebrating the power he has over the lives of the people he betrays, is finally detected and brought to a dungeon, where he ends up chained to a wall - biding his time, waiting for his services to be needed again.<br /><br />"Nothing besides remains..."<br /><br />The allegory on the ugly face of fascism closes with the revitalised people, building a new campanile and celebrating a new start after years of death and destruction. But the dwarf is still alive, and his poisonous tongue is still effectively at work. After all, he has managed to get his simple-minded jailer to bring his journal, so he can continue his account of all-consuming hatred.<br /><br />Fascism might have been chained to a dungeon wall in 1945, Lagerkvist seems to reflect, but it is not dead, and it has not lost its venomous tongue and its will to annihilate whoever celebrates freedom of thought and liberal pleasure in life and art.<br /><br />I read this novel many, many years ago, and it left me puzzled. I picked it up again this week as one of my sons is reading it for class, and I was stunned at the chilling combination of the flawless, perfectly beautiful Swedish prose and the absolute negativity of the message it contains. The beauty of culture, symbolised in Lagerkvist's language, contains the ugliness of fascist thought. At the most powerful and civilised court, hatred can spread when inequality, bullying and social distinctions are the norm. <br /><br />When ancient codes of honour guide military action, any populist whisperer can cause destruction, followed by a flood of refugees and victims of hunger and disease.<br /><br />Beware of unchained hatred!<br /><br />Must-read! A true Nobel Laureate with an eternally relevant message.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3955297714023584363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=classic">classic</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer9386686360124181537"><i>The Dwarf</i> is a strange book, both political in its approach but also much deeper than that. The best way I think I could describe it is that it's about observation and perception, and how a society's collective emotions invariably transfer to even the most silent and optimistic. I'm still not a hundred percent sure I understood fully what point the author was trying to make, but one thing I can say without a doubt is that this is an intriguing book.<br/><br/>It starts off kind of slow and uneventful, seem</span>
  <span id="freeText9386686360124181537" style="display:none"><i>The Dwarf</i> is a strange book, both political in its approach but also much deeper than that. The best way I think I could describe it is that it's about observation and perception, and how a society's collective emotions invariably transfer to even the most silent and optimistic. I'm still not a hundred percent sure I understood fully what point the author was trying to make, but one thing I can say without a doubt is that this is an intriguing book.<br /><br />It starts off kind of slow and uneventful, seeming to just be a fairly typical story, but it soon becomes apparent that its main character has a lot to say about the current world he inhabits. Not only does the book have some great quotes, but its characters and setting were really captivating and vivid, too. I ended up really enjoying it and I can definitely see why it's considered a classic.</span>

<div id="review_914411258" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/914411258" />
    <a title="Kinga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110812-kinga"><img alt="Kinga" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491308194p2/110812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/914411258?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kinga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kinga" href="/user/show/110812-kinga">Kinga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/110812-kinga?shelf=pub-1944">pub-1944</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer914411258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8824896808055273741">The Dwarf is a strange little book written by a Swedish Nobel Prize winner some time during WWII. Itâs a rather allegoric story narrated by a dwarf living on the court in an unnamed Italian city probably in the 15th century, but actually all the hints point us in the same direction as Machiavelliâs Prince who was modelled on Cesare Borgia. In The Dwarf we also find Master Bernardo who is obviously Leonardo da Vinci (and who coincidentally also resided in Cesare Borgiaâs court often.)<br/><br/>The plot is </span>
  <span id="freeText8824896808055273741" style="display:none">The Dwarf is a strange little book written by a Swedish Nobel Prize winner some time during WWII. Itâs a rather allegoric story narrated by a dwarf living on the court in an unnamed Italian city probably in the 15th century, but actually all the hints point us in the same direction as Machiavelliâs Prince who was modelled on Cesare Borgia. In The Dwarf we also find Master Bernardo who is obviously Leonardo da Vinci (and who coincidentally also resided in Cesare Borgiaâs court often.)<br /><br />The plot is rather simple and fable-like, it consists of war, tragic romance and a plague, so really all your renaissance staples. The twist is our little narrator, though. He is so unabashedly evil and full of hate for almost everything and everyone, that I must admit I found it occasionally laughable. The unnamed dwarf finds excitement in conflict and battle. Itâs only then that he can achieve a state that could be described as happiness. He takes no joy in food and finds the idea of sex absolutely repulsive. He is the agent provocateur and the catalyst for all the terrible decisions that the Prince (and some other characters) make. He schemes and plots always aiming for the worst possible outcome. <br /><br />Of course, you donât need a PhD in literature to figure out that this is all a metaphor for a little dwarf that lives inside all of us. That little creature that pushes us to do despicable things for the thrill of it (apparently). Itâs what's little in us that really rules, the smallest and pettiest. Itâs not even the sophisticated kind of Machiavellian evildoing; his outbursts are more like toddler tantrums â "I donât understand it therefore I hate it and want it gone". Generally, itâs a rather sad summary of human condition but then it was written when it still looked like Hitler might win, so who could blame Lagerkvist for being a little disillusioned with the humanity. <br /><br />Personally, I found this metaphor rather heavy-handed and not particularly original or thought-provoking. Someone at my book club mentioned that it was quite representative of Swedish (or Scandinavian in general) fiction not to strive for ambitious symbolism but settle for the very obvious. This, of course, is completely anecdotal so do not argue with me in the comments, but apparently itâs perfectly acceptable and apparently lauded even by critics (hence the Nobel Prize).<br /> <br />Having said all that, I must admit some of the paragraphs were quite amusing and I wish I could quote them. Alas, I read the book in its Polish translation and if I translated it to English now we would probably end up with something spectacularly different from what it was originally in Swedish. Overall, the narrator finds humans confusing and most of the time simply idiotic. His observations could be compared to those of a child trying to understand an adult world by herself or an alien trying to make sense of the life of on Earth. The Dwarf doesnât understand why humans are convinced there is some greatness to life, he is bemused by how quickly people move between enthusiasm and joy and hopelessness and despair and he is forever baffled by the idea of physical love or any other kind of love for that matter. And what he doesn't understand, he hates. Simple as that.<br /><br />And now, to wrap it up, let me get all PC on you. Was I really the only person made slightly uncomfortable by this idea that âdwarvesâ are some sort different non-human species and representing all the evil inside all of us? Yes, a metaphor of course. But if I were a little person reading this book, I think I would be pretty damn pissed off to be the butt of that metaphor. This was written during the reign of Hitler and letâs not forget what he had in store for people with disabilities.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8824896808055273741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1543599284" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1543599284" />
    <a title="Dhanaraj Rajan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan"><img alt="Dhanaraj Rajan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433251321p2/4373783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1543599284?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dhanaraj Rajan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dhanaraj Rajan" href="/user/show/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan">Dhanaraj Rajan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780809013036" href="/book/show/2604985-the-dwarf">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=scandinavian-lit">scandinavian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=nobel-prize">nobel-prize</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1543599284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14510290487615603391">May be 4 and half stars.<br/><br/>I can speak much about this novel. That itself is a warning I give to myself. For I will be tending to blabber and bore the hell out of the reader. So instead, let me try to be precise with my observations and impressions by ordering them in neat points.<br/><br/>1. This is my fifth book by Lagerkvist. And unlike the other books, the theme of religion is not extensively treated in this one. I love Lagerkvist for his thoughts on religion. This novel bears some jottings on religion. </span>
  <span id="freeText14510290487615603391" style="display:none">May be 4 and half stars.<br /><br />I can speak much about this novel. That itself is a warning I give to myself. For I will be tending to blabber and bore the hell out of the reader. So instead, let me try to be precise with my observations and impressions by ordering them in neat points.<br /><br />1. This is my fifth book by Lagerkvist. And unlike the other books, the theme of religion is not extensively treated in this one. I love Lagerkvist for his thoughts on religion. This novel bears some jottings on religion. But then when compared with his other works, the jottings are bare minimum.<br /><br />2. This is a book that contains many allusions.<br /><br />3. For instance, this book was written when the World War II was at its peak. This novel can be seen as an allegory of the World Wars. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[This novel is set in the 15th century Italy (probably in Milan or Florence or an imagined place that fuses together Milan-Florence) where feudalism and factionalism reigned supreme. The king of this place is addressed all through the novel as Prince with capital P. He has among his many loyal subjects/servants a dwarf who loathes human beings. He considers himself a superior being. He assumes that he knows the innermost secrets of human beings, and especially that of his master - the Prince. He wages a war with his enemy. The war ends up a failure. So by deceit he kills the enemy. The enemy nation now vows to revenge its leader's death. They wage war on the Prince. The Prince's army is defeated and everyone fears the day when their city will be attacked. Meanwhile many had flood to the capital city seeking refuge. The city is under siege. The war comes to an end with the onset of the plague. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Here is an excerpt on the war by the dwarf, who also likes to record everything. In fact, the whole novel is in the form of dwarf's recording of events. Here it goes: <b>"And so, the plague has put an end to the war as nothing else could have done. Both countries are pillaged, particularly our own. The populations are probably too exhausted after two wars to be able to go on." </b> Though simple in its observation, at the end of this novel reading such comment invariably reminds the reader of the two World Wars in quick succession and its horrific effects.<br /><br />4. As it is said in the spoiler, the king of the place is always addressed as the Prince with the capital P. That is another important allusion to Niccolo Machiavelli's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/393134.The_Prince" title="The Prince by NiccolÃ² Machiavelli" rel="nofollow noopener">The Prince</a>. It seems to be a political book that alludes to Hitler in Nazi Germany. This allusion lends the work all the more historically important. <br /><br />5. It is at this stage important to note that the dwarf who is the servant of the Prince is also alluded to be the dark side of the Prince. The dwarf stands for the dark secret of any human being in general. What is the dark secret? Or that which every human being harbours in his inner most self? Lagerkvist states that it is the hatred for one's own race and love of violence. Now, once again connect the figure of Hitler with the Prince and Hitler's desire to rule over the world with the Prince's waging war to destroy his enemy. The picture is complete. On war, the following is the dwarf's opinion: <b>"Enemy country is enemy country and must be treated as such. That is the law of war. It may be cruel, but war and cruelty go together; there is no getting away from it. One must exterminate the people against whom one is fighting and ravage the land so that it cannot rise again. It can be very dangerous t leave a few opposition centres in the rear; one must know that one's back is free."</b><br /><br />It is also to be noted that whenever the dwarf records the events of war, he records it with such ecstasy.<br /><br />6. This is also a novel about the psychological <i>id</i> - the base desire. The base desire is the desire to kill the other, especially the one belonging to your race. The <i>Id</i> is present in everyone. Only that everyone likes to hide it at the innermost dungeons of his/her soul. That is what happens at last to the dwarf in the novel. He is feared by everyone and held to be responsible for all the events in the city. So he is arrested and taken to the deepest cell in the dungeon of the castle and is fastened to the wall by chains. Here is the best ever monologue of the dwarf at this time: <br /><br /><b>"What does it matter if I sit here in the dungeon? What good does it do if they clap me in irons? I still belong to the castle just as much as before! To prove it they have even welded me to it! We are forged together, it and I! We cannot escape from each other, my master and I! If I am imprisoned, then he is imprisoned too! If I am linked to him, then he too is linked to me! Here I am in my hole, living my obscure mole life, while he goes about in his fine handsome halls. But my life is also his, and his noble highly respectable life up there really belongs to me."</b><br /><br />Final Statement: A powerful novel with many number of allusions. Let the reader also be aware that I have left unsaid many other aspects of this book. For instance, the dwarf's sarcastic take on love and peace; and religion and faith.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14510290487615603391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristen" href="/user/show/1856764-kristen">Kristen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who enjoy the smell of methadone and sadness in the morning </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1856764-kristen?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1856764-kristen?shelf=make-you-put-a-gun-in-your-mouth">make-you-put-a-gun-in-your-mouth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1856764-kristen?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer450803401" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15599508338159540199">This sure feels like the type of book I would love: incredibly dark, misanthropic, (pseudo)philosophical. The dwarf Piccoline is one of the most miserably hateful characters I have encountered. Yet rather than luxuriating in his own hate, and allowing the reader to do the same, his evil is more a lack of humanity, his motivation as utilitarian as the authorâs prose. Far from the anti-hero Underground Man, or the delicious villain Iago, Piccoline is somehow evil and completely indifferent. And pe</span>
  <span id="freeText15599508338159540199" style="display:none">This sure feels like the type of book I would love: incredibly dark, misanthropic, (pseudo)philosophical. The dwarf Piccoline is one of the most miserably hateful characters I have encountered. Yet rather than luxuriating in his own hate, and allowing the reader to do the same, his evil is more a lack of humanity, his motivation as utilitarian as the authorâs prose. Far from the anti-hero Underground Man, or the delicious villain Iago, Piccoline is somehow evil and completely indifferent. And perhaps thatâs why I felt I couldnât really sink my teeth into this short novel. Personally, I love characters that kill kittens, commit murders, and talk people into offing themselves, yet somehow the Dwarf just doesnât do it for me. But maybe it's just me? <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15599508338159540199" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer205825055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7259168872184731265">Bleakly narrated by one of the more memorably nihilistic characters in modern literature, The Dwarf is a tale of struggles of an Italian medieval city-state as it withers from within. The plot points, war and intrigue, could come from any number of other works set in this period, and move the story briskly along, but the desolate philosophy underlying the action holds a singular fascination and weight. Filled with memorable epigrams for a doomed humanity. I'm not sure when this was written, but </span>
  <span id="freeText7259168872184731265" style="display:none">Bleakly narrated by one of the more memorably nihilistic characters in modern literature, The Dwarf is a tale of struggles of an Italian medieval city-state as it withers from within. The plot points, war and intrigue, could come from any number of other works set in this period, and move the story briskly along, but the desolate philosophy underlying the action holds a singular fascination and weight. Filled with memorable epigrams for a doomed humanity. I'm not sure when this was written, but published in 1945, it seems apt that its composition seems to have coincided with WWII.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer275396109080153835">I've never read anything by Lagerkvist, but knew he had won the Nobel Prize for Literature in the early 1950s. I was browsing the shelves at my public library and decided to check him out. I ddn't know what his other writing is like, but this was a really amazing book. It's only about 200 pages and his style allows you to breeze through the narrative quickly. I would describe the story as something like a fable, told from the perspective of a 26 inch dwarf who is something like a "fool"-type cha</span>
  <span id="freeText275396109080153835" style="display:none">I've never read anything by Lagerkvist, but knew he had won the Nobel Prize for Literature in the early 1950s. I was browsing the shelves at my public library and decided to check him out. I ddn't know what his other writing is like, but this was a really amazing book. It's only about 200 pages and his style allows you to breeze through the narrative quickly. I would describe the story as something like a fable, told from the perspective of a 26 inch dwarf who is something like a "fool"-type character to a Prince during the Renaissance period in Italy. If you're interested in the subject of the presence of "evil" in human nature, this is the book for you. On the surface, it's really a simply-told story that teenagers might like, but I can't stop thinking about it days later. I found it to be a really chilling tale of the dark part of our nature. Makes me want to read more of this author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="275396109080153835" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer12913702869782474266"><i> "And they are deformed though it does not show on the outside. I live only my dwarf life. I never go around tall and smooth-featured. I am ever myself, always the same, I live one life alone. I have no other being inside me. And I recognize everything within me, nothing ever comes up from my inner depths, nothing there is shrouded in mystery. Therefore I do not fear the things which frighten them, the incoherent, the unknown, the mysterious. Such things do not exist for me. There is nothing "di</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12913702869782474266" style="display:none"><i> "And they are deformed though it does not show on the outside. I live only my dwarf life. I never go around tall and smooth-featured. I am ever myself, always the same, I live one life alone. I have no other being inside me. And I recognize everything within me, nothing ever comes up from my inner depths, nothing there is shrouded in mystery. Therefore I do not fear the things which frighten them, the incoherent, the unknown, the mysterious. Such things do not exist for me. There is nothing "different" about me."</i><br /><br />Dostovesky warns in Notes From Underground that utopias can never be created as there will always be some people who just crave for destruction. The titular and nameless dwarf here seems to one such creature. There are some reasons to believe that he show Prince's id, even the above quote could be read something to the effect - but that doesn't seem to be neccessarily true. The id of a person is among other things hungry for all things material - dwarf shows no love for anything more than food neccessary for his survival.<br /><br />There is though a strong connection between two. Dwarf himself stresses on it repeatedly. And they are similarly excited about attacking and uninterested in defending, they are both loyal in their own strange ways to the princess. Is he his evil half, now being put in dark and chains? <br /><br />It seems difficult to read it an allegory of war too. Apart from fact that all evil dwarfs seems to be maraphors of Hitler and it was written near the end of world war. To be honest, making dwarfs metaphor of Hitler seems a bit lazy.<br /><br />The artist in the book seems to be very close Leonardo Da Vinci - his two works similar to Mona Lisa and The Last Supper. Again I don't know what PL was trying to say about Vinci. To be short, I understood nothing</span>

<div id="review_30290606" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30290606" />
    <a title="khashayar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8275-khashayar"><img alt="khashayar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280345967p2/8275.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30290606?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="khashayar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="khashayar" href="/user/show/8275-khashayar">khashayar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30290606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2820798500486571295">The Dwarf is indeed an intriguing masterpiece which can nicely depict the human dilemma of virtue and evil. Set in an unnamed Italian city-state in the Renaissance, the story is narrated in the Piccoline's diary. Piccoline is a dwarf who serves the Prince at the court, and knows no emotions but hatred, violence, and malice. He impersonates a true misanthrope who can respect only the dark side of the humanity; yet, is in no rash to befriend every evil-hearted he meets in the court. Don Ricardo, d</span>
  <span id="freeText2820798500486571295" style="display:none">The Dwarf is indeed an intriguing masterpiece which can nicely depict the human dilemma of virtue and evil. Set in an unnamed Italian city-state in the Renaissance, the story is narrated in the Piccoline's diary. Piccoline is a dwarf who serves the Prince at the court, and knows no emotions but hatred, violence, and malice. He impersonates a true misanthrope who can respect only the dark side of the humanity; yet, is in no rash to befriend every evil-hearted he meets in the court. Don Ricardo, despite his despicable and wicked character, is fervently hated by the dwarf, and is finally poisoned by him. <br /><br />The story can be read as a distorted historical novel depicting the turmoil of the Renaissance, the deceit of the duchies, and the violence of the mercenaries, but is rather a symbolic story of human nature, particularly the eternal and internal struggle between good and evil. The novel symbolically yet masterly expounds the human nature. In an extroversive manner, various human reactions, in face of disastrous difficulties e.g. war, plague, and famine, are depicted throughout the novel. In an introversive manner; on the other hand, Piccoline could be thought as a dwarf who lives inside every human being. This internal dwarf can be kept chained in the abyss of the soul but manages to rise up and frisk about. This is what Piccoline suggests somewhere in his diary. Ironically, he is sure of his freedom because 'soon the Prince will need his dwarf again'.<br /><br />I certainly recommend the book but I would have liked it much better if it were set on one of the truthful, historical events of the Italian Wars. Under the current circumstances, the Prince could be either Ludovico Sforza or Cesare Borgia and the city-state could be either Milan or Florence. If you are not an expert of Italian Renaissance then you are further lost in who's who.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2820798500486571295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_467499852" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/467499852" />
    <a title="Yair Ben-Zvi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3223578-yair-ben-zvi"><img alt="Yair Ben-Zvi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588137043p2/3223578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/467499852?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yair Ben-Zvi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yair Ben-Zvi" href="/user/show/3223578-yair-ben-zvi">Yair Ben-Zvi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780809013036" href="/book/show/2604985-the-dwarf">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3223578-yair-ben-zvi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer467499852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4400548151341590279">I very much enjoyed this novel for, among other things, its relentlessly bleak and darkly comic (by way of the grotesque) tone. This is a story that just. doesn't. give. a. damn. about your feelings concerning the events described or the characters partaking in said events....it simply presents them (adroitly if also matched by concomitant bitterness) as they are and leaves you to judge them, in a sense, purely. No consideration is given to modulation or amelioration of darkness, it's a dark lit</span>
  <span id="freeText4400548151341590279" style="display:none">I very much enjoyed this novel for, among other things, its relentlessly bleak and darkly comic (by way of the grotesque) tone. This is a story that just. doesn't. give. a. damn. about your feelings concerning the events described or the characters partaking in said events....it simply presents them (adroitly if also matched by concomitant bitterness) as they are and leaves you to judge them, in a sense, purely. No consideration is given to modulation or amelioration of darkness, it's a dark little treatise on the fallibility and numerous (beyond that into the realm of multifarious) sins and weaknesses of the human condition. This is a novel for those with a bit more iron than normal in their blood...but it does pay back, in dividends even.<br /><br />If nothing else, it's assiduously honest. And in this age of uber sensitivity and pseudo-intellectual soundbites being palmed off as dogma, every conceivable side, readers can use this kind of bracing transparency. It's not a perfect novel, however. What keeps this novel from transcending (or 'full bloom' if you'll indulge me) is that it's too damn short. Don't crucify me too severely for playing the 'authorial intent' card but it's almost as if Par Lagerkvist either lost his enthusiasm for the story, maybe had some of his nerve diminished (or exhausted) or, very likely, he meant it this way. And if the final possibility is the case then I am at least grateful for the jarring (and jagged) black diamond of a novel(lla) we have. I just wanted more of that honesty.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4400548151341590279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2693251426" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2693251426" />
    <a title="Lia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44005555-lia"><img alt="Lia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578446760p2/44005555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2693251426?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lia" href="/user/show/44005555-lia">Lia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44005555-lia?shelf=nobel-lit">nobel-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44005555-lia?shelf=ugly-stuff">ugly-stuff</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2693251426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7348982881896361055">This is my third Lagerkvist, the more I read, the more I like them. <br/><br/>Individual agents in Lagerkvistâs books seem to also work as metaphorical vehicles to deliver, to question, to explore otherwise esoteric, ethereal concepts. Concepts like what it means to be human, whatâs hidden and unsaid within us, what it means to believe, what is love, etc.<br/><br/>If Barabbas the unbeliever was physically chained to a believing-martyr, the Dwarf is figuratively chained to his exact opposite. If the Prince was power</span>
  <span id="freeText7348982881896361055" style="display:none">This is my third Lagerkvist, the more I read, the more I like them. <br /><br />Individual agents in Lagerkvistâs books seem to also work as metaphorical vehicles to deliver, to question, to explore otherwise esoteric, ethereal concepts. Concepts like what it means to be human, whatâs hidden and unsaid within us, what it means to believe, what is love, etc.<br /><br />If Barabbas the unbeliever was physically chained to a believing-martyr, the Dwarf is figuratively chained to his exact opposite. If the Prince was powerful, the Dwarf was the least, the most powerless, the most mocked, scorned, ignored, unrecognized. If the Prince presents the world with the most acceptable facade, the most cordial appearance, our narrator-dwarf gives us the most brutally contemptuous account of the same events, the same people. If the Prince neglects his wife; the dwarf grudgingly entertains her, listens to her secrets, her confessions. If the Prince suppressed his anger and holds back his wrath; the dwarf kills them without being asked to.<br /><br />The Dwarf is the egoistic Princeâs id; the Hyde of Dr. Jekyll, the unacceptable creature that Dr. Frankenstein materialized.<br /><br />It is interesting that Lagerkvist not-so-subtly alludes to Da Vinci, Madonna Litta (or was it Mona Lisa), to the Christ even â and shows us the humiliating, indulgent, violent, dark side of them. The point seems to be that we are only shown the presentable facade, at the core of these saintly icons, thereâs always something ugly that gets excluded in their portraits.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7348982881896361055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1510259851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16208220976669042294">A clever, brilliantly executed look into the malice and selfishness of humans. Peculiar, but brilliant.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1307600238" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1307600238" />
    <a title="Bbrown" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6261192-bbrown"><img alt="Bbrown" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404741580p2/6261192.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1307600238?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bbrown" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bbrown" href="/user/show/6261192-bbrown">Bbrown</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1307600238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11760123086110892563">The narrator and titular character of The Dwarf is a character so purely evil and unlikable that he serves as a refutation of the very worldview he espouses, even without any other character presenting an explicit counterargument or different perspective. The glee that the dwarf takes at pain, violence, and death is disgusting, and throughout the course of this short book he partakes in everything from animal cruelty to murder to false accusations of rape. At one point the dwarf muses: <br/><blockquote>Therefore</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11760123086110892563" style="display:none">The narrator and titular character of The Dwarf is a character so purely evil and unlikable that he serves as a refutation of the very worldview he espouses, even without any other character presenting an explicit counterargument or different perspective. The glee that the dwarf takes at pain, violence, and death is disgusting, and throughout the course of this short book he partakes in everything from animal cruelty to murder to false accusations of rape. At one point the dwarf muses: <br /><blockquote>Therefore everything is really quite futile. Of course it is. What would life be like if it were not futile? Futility is the foundation upon which it rests. On what other foundation could it have been based which would have held and never given way? A great idea can be undermined by another great idea and, in due course, be demolished by it. But futility is inaccessible, indestructible, immovable. It is a true foundation and that is why it has been chosen as such. That so much cogitation should be required to realize that! I know that by instinct. It is my nature to know it. </blockquote><br />With such an unadulterated villain espousing this view, itâs clear that Lagerkvist believes just the opposite, and wants his readers to come to that conclusion as well.<br /><br />Beyond providing a withering critique of sadism and nihilism, there really isnât much to The Dwarf. Itâs set in an ahistorical Italian city-state that features a fictionalized version of Leonardo da Vinci, though none of the other characters or events have clear historical parallels that I know of. The book tells the story of the prince of that city-state, who the dwarf admires and is relatedly one of the most despicable characters of the work, seeing as heâs a warmongering liar. There is a strong focus on sexuality, from innocent first love to utter debauchery, and the dwarfâs sexuality is particularly enigmatic. I donât really have much else to say about it.<br /><br />The main purpose of The Dwarf is to provide an example of what not to do and what mindset not to have. This Lagerkvist does well, without preaching to his readers. The book, however, does very little else.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11760123086110892563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It is a book about the savage pointlessness of war and the rationalizations through which human beings attribute evil deeds to supposedly righteous intentions.   <span id="freeText9053043901935187869" style="display:none">Itâs no surprise that this book was written at the end of World War II in Europe, a continent torn apart by war and evil. The Dwarf is set some 500 years earlier, in a Princeâs court in a city-state in Renaissance Italy. It is a book about the savage pointlessness of war and the rationalizations through which human beings attribute evil deeds to supposedly righteous intentions. It is a quick and unforgettable read, as dark as the heart of the unnamed dwarf who narrates this classic by Nobel Prize winner Per Lagerkvist. Hereâs one of the less nihilistic quotes from the book:<br /><br /> One for whom a pebble has value must be surrounded by treasures wherever he goes.</span>

<div id="review_746224957" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/746224957" />
    <a title="Gabriel Valjan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11940358-gabriel-valjan"><img alt="Gabriel Valjan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455903791p2/11940358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/746224957?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabriel Valjan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabriel Valjan" href="/user/show/11940358-gabriel-valjan">Gabriel Valjan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer746224957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16435217523949474761">In 1944, with World War II in the background, the Swedish writer PÃ¤r Lagerkvist composed The Dwarf (DvÃ¤rgen), which Alexandra Dick translated for the English-speaking world in 1945. Known for slim, allegorical novels, and simple, direct language, often reading like a fairy tale, Lagerkvist created one of the most evil and compelling characters in world literature: Piccoline the dwarf. Lagerkvistâs Dwarf is his warning about any -ism, any ideology.<br/><br/>A Swede commenting on political power should give</span>
  <span id="freeText16435217523949474761" style="display:none">In 1944, with World War II in the background, the Swedish writer PÃ¤r Lagerkvist composed The Dwarf (DvÃ¤rgen), which Alexandra Dick translated for the English-speaking world in 1945. Known for slim, allegorical novels, and simple, direct language, often reading like a fairy tale, Lagerkvist created one of the most evil and compelling characters in world literature: Piccoline the dwarf. Lagerkvistâs Dwarf is his warning about any -ism, any ideology.<br /><br />A Swede commenting on political power should give pause. Sweden, though it has maintained neutrality since 1815, exhibited what could only be called schizophrenic behavior, for and against both sides of the conflict in World War II. For instance, Sweden allowed the Wehrmacht to use the countryâs railways to invade Norway and Finland, yet provided refuge to nearly all of Denmarkâs expelled Jews, some Norwegian Jews, and collaborated with Allied air forces by granting then airbase space. Sweden harbored a teeming Nazi movement (remember the first book of the Millennium trilogy?) and yet had heroes like Raoul Wallenberg. Lagerkvist may have been writing The Dwarf with a sense of guilt.<br /><br />Piccoline states at the start of the novel that he is not human; he is a dwarf, an older tribe of life in our world, born misshapen and old at birth. He is a physical aberration, outside of Nature. For an additional touch, Lagerkvistâs dwarf is a redhead. Redheads, more often than not, have had a bad reputation in history and literature, associated with Judaism, with Barbarossa, Elizabeth I, Judas, Lenin, Malcolm X, Mordred, and Napoleon. Piccoline is not a Diane Arbus oddity. He is a terrifying figure.<br /><br />Lagerkvist situates the story at an Italian court, in the Renaissance period. The author turned to that period in history because it had created the nascent modern political state and offered a model for political leadership: the Prince. The Church was still active but declining in prestige and influence. Both Eliot and D.H. Lawrence would agree on this point. Secularism was on the rise. The Machiavellian prince had become the model of a good leader because he inspired fear and because he was respected for his gifts of deception and manipulation. Piccoline is the princeâs right-hand man.<br /><br />Piccoline lives to serve his prince. The head of State must maintain power. Piccoline is, in essence, a capable bureaucrat, a predecessor of the efficient bureaucrats of the Holocaust. The prince denies culpability and the few times that Piccoline is thought excessive he is punished, but never executed: the prince knows better. Piccoline is too good at what he does, serving the prince's purposes. Heâll go away, spend some time in chains, but then he'll return, more poisonous and more spiteful than ever. If Piccoline is evil and the prince employs his service often, then isnât the prince evil? Arenât evil acts a political necessity? The Dwarf is a series of journal entries into which the reader is led and, as the Dwarf himself might be pleased to know, sadist that he is, entertained by unthinkable and unspeakable acts of terror and mayhem.<br /><br />The reader eats it all up, page after page of a sociopathâs adventures. We are bourgeois, consumers of mass culture, including its literature. Lagerkvist is illustrating Hermann Broch's idea of mass psychosis. We have become consumers of graven images and ideas. Hatred consumes Piccoline and we consume him for entertainment. âConsumerâ is an awful word. It is a horrible concept. âConsumerâ equates the human being to a biblical plague, like grasshoppers in the Pharaoh's fields. We accept âconsumersâ in business language and popular culture without so much as raising an eyebrow. âConsumptionâ is seen as a positive value. Marketing is the pseudoscience of human desire.<br /><br />We like Evil. Lagerkvist shows that the State and Individual are capable of evil. We condone and justify ânecessary murder.â Evil fascinates us in literature and movies. We might say that we like to see Good triumph at the end, but the truth is, Evil is far more engaging and entertaining. âBeautÃ© du Diable,â as the French would say. Evil is erotic, fantasy, and wish fulfillment. Eliot and modernists would have diagnosed this aesthetic as corrupt and pathological. Vampires and werewolves, for instance, were frightening because they were undead, incapable of final rest. We have made vampires and werewolves dynastic; weâve made them misunderstood creatures, full of wisdom, intriguing to us day-walkers. The bald and bat-eared Nosferatu of 1922 is the great-great grandfather of Joss Whedonâs Angel and Spike.<br /><br />Good is banal and boring; Evil, dark and enticing. Danteâs Inferno is far more interesting than Paradiso; and that is what is wrong about âmodern culture.â Walt Whitman, the precursor to modernist poetry, is bold and bombastic, until he starts talking about death. Emily Dickinson, otherwise a cipher, has our ears and rivets us when she talks about Death. French literature is stuffy and tedious, until Baudelaire shows the reader that Paris is a necropolis, full of decay and decadence. We like Evil. We like serial killers and the pathology of a mind utterly unhinged. We want to hear what Hannibal Lechter says, as long as he is behind glass. Does Art reflect reality and culture? Is this our interior space?<br /><br />Quite simply, the concept of the undead in popular culture is metaphorical. We are the undead. We are the automatons, the electronic sheep, the sleepwalkers whose sole purpose is to be brand-loyal consumers once we leave the multiplex. Art (often unconsciously) tells us that the king is naked. We laugh, find a momentary recognition of reality, but we end up dismissing it as entertainment, as fiction. The Matrix informs us that we have been sleeping inside a cocoon. We come out of the theater and return to the cocoon. No awakening. No enlightenment. There was a reason why George Romero ended Dawn of the Dead (1978) in a mall.<br /><br />Literature has had numerous demons. Authors, for artistic purposes, have demonized feminine sexuality, skin color, ethnicity: Catholics as Papists, Jews, Italians, and Poles, but sexuality can be suppressed and ethnicity, hidden. Changing skin color is difficult, but not impossible. A dwarf is undeniable. Not all dwarfs are evil, though. Oskar Matzerath in GÃ¼nter Grassâs The Tin Drum is clinically insane, but not evil. Ursula Hegiâs Trudi Montag in Stones from the River is difficult, but heroic. George R. R. Martinâs Tyrion in the A Song of Ice and Fire series is cagey and wily, but he is by necessity. Lagerkvistâs Piccoline is evil. He knows it and takes delight in it. In The Dwarf, evil rests in chains; not vanquished, but waiting, like Miltonâs Satan, or the Titans in classical mythology. Patient, seething Piccoline waits to serve again, knowing that his prince will need him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16435217523949474761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5949738-karl?shelf=nobel-reading-project">nobel-reading-project</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5949738-karl?shelf=my-all-time-favourite-books">my-all-time-favourite-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5949738-karl?shelf=scandinavian-and-nordic">scandinavian-and-nordic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer198346426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8842752293325327829">Written in clear, concise prose, Nobel Prize winner Par Lagerkvist's The Dwarf is a richly philosophical novel, that offers perceptive insights on a host of topics from personal identity to religion to war and even romantic relationships. Like all great first-person narrators in fiction, Piccoline The Dwarf is an excellent choice due to his status as an outsider in the Prince's court, he sees much and has a lot to say.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14840677686797008103">The Dwarf observes. He judges. He despises. And he hates.<br/><blockquote>
  <i>It is difficult to understand those whom one does not hate, for then one is unarmed, one has nothing with which to penetrate into their being.</i>
</blockquote><br/>The familiar tautology, <i>Haters gonna hate</i>, does not do justice to the bile which bubbles from this narratorâs pen and mouth.   <span id="freeText14840677686797008103" style="display:none">The Dwarf observes. He judges. He despises. And he hates.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>It is difficult to understand those whom one does not hate, for then one is unarmed, one has nothing with which to penetrate into their being.</i>
</blockquote><br />The familiar tautology, <i>Haters gonna hate</i>, does not do justice to the bile which bubbles from this narratorâs pen and mouth. He loathes the weakness of humans, the false faces they present socially, their enslavement to desire and affection, their very existence. His character operates like a physical embodiment of the death drive or thanatos. Humanâs self-destructive nature in overdrive:<blockquote>
  <i>It is my fate that I hate my own people. My race is detestable to me.<br /> But I hate myself too. I eat my own splenetic flesh. I drink my own poisoned blood. Every day I perform my solitary communion as the grim high priest of my people.</i>
</blockquote><br />Despite all this negativity, the Dwarf does esteem the Prince. For his standing, for the times he shows reserve and poise, for his power. The Dwarf admires manliness, dismisses almost every female in the book, and revels in the feeling of purpose and agency war delivers the individual.<br /><br />And so the royal palace and all its happenings filters through this distorted, maligned, and mocked Otherâs perspective. His is a role of trusted servant who manages to sow more discord and chaos than any single human in the book, juxtaposed against the hope and meaning promised by religion, love, art, and culture. Lagerkvist gives the reader a kind of base masculinity stripped of any human positives. Like looking into a funhouse mirror for too long, our so-called ânormalâ reflection never quite looks the same.<br />-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------<br />WORDS I HAD TO LOOK UP WHILE (OK, AFTER) READING THIS BOOK<br /><a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">pasquinade</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">condottiere</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">campanile</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">grapnels</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">culverins</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">damigella</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">cameriere</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">fal-lals</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">equerries</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">flummery</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">majolica</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">ewers</a> | scalci (anybody know what this word means?) | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">perorate</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">potations</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">extirpation</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">postern</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">prie-dieu</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">orison</a> | <a href="â" rel="nofollow noopener">habilaments</a> <br /></span>

<div id="review_2360402795" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2360402795" />
    <a title="Bonnie Wilson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57683698-bonnie-wilson"><img alt="Bonnie Wilson" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2360402795?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bonnie Wilson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bonnie Wilson" href="/user/show/57683698-bonnie-wilson">Bonnie Wilson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2360402795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5304856990039420082">Once in a while, I think I should read something that is not "just" for entertainment and distraction. The author was a 1951 Nobel Prize winner and the jacket says that "The Dwarf" (1945) has been "long considered one of the masterpieces of modern literature." Well, this dose will last me a good while - maybe forever. Mercy! What an awful book. The only way I could get through it was to force myself to read a few pages while reading other books - including "Pride and Prejudice" purely as a resto</span>
  <span id="freeText5304856990039420082" style="display:none">Once in a while, I think I should read something that is not "just" for entertainment and distraction. The author was a 1951 Nobel Prize winner and the jacket says that "The Dwarf" (1945) has been "long considered one of the masterpieces of modern literature." Well, this dose will last me a good while - maybe forever. Mercy! What an awful book. The only way I could get through it was to force myself to read a few pages while reading other books - including "Pride and Prejudice" purely as a restorative. I read the Brittanica entry on Lagerkvist before I started the book - an anti-fascist and sounds generally quite admirable so I am really sorry to dislike this book so much. <br /><br />The dwarf is the property of "the Prince" who has echoes of Machiavelli's Prince (and maybe Cesare Borgia) and his aging, sex-obsessed Princess. The three of them are repellent, deranged, and actively evil. There is an artist who is obviously based on Da Vinci who mumbles and blithers about the futility of action while crafting engines of war. Some minor characters who exist to be killed. But none of that is my problem with the book. I am not fond of "triumph of the human spirit/love conquers all/people are inherently good" genre. My issue is that it is boring, repetitive, and the main character's monologues totally unbelievable given his history. <br /><br />This is proclaimed a "philosophical" novel but there is nothing here to really provoke contemplation or analysis. So people who are brutalized, oppressed, and powerless usually act brutally themselves given the chance? So people with virtually unlimited power become almost unlimitedly brutal and evil? So the Lords and Ladies inevitably exploit and kill the populace without a qualm? This is startling or provocative? I think not. <br /><br />My recommendation is to just not bother with this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5304856990039420082" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16287424401170986123" style="display:none">I was going to describe this as an allegorical novel, but I think itâs more accurate to drop the ânovelâ part. Lagerkvistâs message is that everyone has a dark side - hardly world-bending stuff. <div id="review_684998090" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/684998090" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18948588-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569598891p2/18948588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/684998090?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/18948588-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=goodbye">goodbye</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=r-r">r-r</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=three-star-no">three-star-no</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer684998090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7293972818110384276">The Dwarf is a 1944 novel by PÃ¤r Lagerkvist. It was translated into English in 1945, and yes I read the English version. Lagerkvist situates the story at an Italian city, in the Renaissance period. It is not made clear where this city is but from the introduction in my book it seems it was either Milan or Florence, it matters not at all to me since I know nothing about either city. It probably won't give anything away to tell you that the main character in the novel is a dwarf.<br/><br/>Our dwarf's name i</span>
  <span id="freeText7293972818110384276" style="display:none">The Dwarf is a 1944 novel by PÃ¤r Lagerkvist. It was translated into English in 1945, and yes I read the English version. Lagerkvist situates the story at an Italian city, in the Renaissance period. It is not made clear where this city is but from the introduction in my book it seems it was either Milan or Florence, it matters not at all to me since I know nothing about either city. It probably won't give anything away to tell you that the main character in the novel is a dwarf.<br /><br />Our dwarf's name is Piccoline and he tells us in the first few pages that he is 26" tall, shapely and well proportioned, with red hair not black; which leads me to believe that most dwarfs hair is black, but I really don't know, his eyebrows meet and his body strength is considerable. Also he never laughs, he is no buffoon, he is a dwarf and nothing but a dwarf. According to Piccoline dwarfs are descended from a race older than that which now populates the world and he has nothing against belonging to a different race from the present one. <br /><br />Piccoline is devoted to the Prince. According to our dwarf the Prince is an imposing personality, he gives the impression of being able to understand anything, he is a great and powerful man, he is the only person that Piccoline does not despise. As Piccoline says <i>"I follow him constantly, like a shadow."</i> If the Prince ever gets a name I missed it.<br /><br />The Princess does have a name, Princess Teodora; and Piccoline is being truthful when he says he hates everyone else, he certainly seems to hate the Princess. In his words:<br /><br /><i>"I hate her and I should like to see her dead, burning in the fires of hell with her legs astraddle and the flames licking her foul belly."</i><br /><br />The Princess apparently has had many lovers and Piccoline hates them all. He says:<br /><br /><i>"I hate all her lvoers. I have wanted to fling myself upon every one of them and pierce them with my dagger to see their blood flow."</i><br /><br />His hatred seems especially directed to her one lover, Don Riccardo; he finds him repulsive. He hates the Princes's daughter Angelica, it seems because she used to make him play with her when she was a young girl. He hates other dwarfs, he says of them:<br /><br /><i>"They are a contemptible clan. So that I need not see them I have made the Prince sell all the dwarfs here, one after the other, until I am the only one at the court. I am glad they are gone...."</i><br /><br />And he hates himself: <i>"It is my fate that I hate my own people. My race is detestable to me. But I hate myself too."</i><br /><br />Piccoline tells us all this in page after page of diary entries. Keeping a diary myself for years I found it strange that his entries are never dated. The thoughts and emotions of the dwarf are shocking, evil and filled with hatred. In fact I could go on and on about the people and things hated by Piccoline but I am tired of thinking of them. The story centers on the feud and war between the Prince and his people and a neighboring state. The dwarf loves it, he loves the war, the killing, the mayhem. He is furious when he finds that a peace treaty is going to be signed. He is proud of having killed a man during the war, even though it is an unarmed dwarf. <br /><br />I've read that this novel was Lagerkvist's attempt at creating a genuinely evil character illustrating the evil sides of man. I don't know if that's true, I don't read novels and try to figure out the deeper meanings behind them, I read novels because I enjoy reading, but if it is true that he was illustrating the evil sides of man, I hope he failed, I hope we're not as bad as the dwarf. Happy reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7293972818110384276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Sophie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1073899-sophie"><img alt="Sophie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403647332p2/1073899.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a title="Sophie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sophie" href="/user/show/1073899-sophie">Sophie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97317064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12673708143684108890">The first person narration of the story told through constant journal entries became a little forced at times; Lagerkvist gave context and a sense of time to each entry which made it feel artificial. The structure reminds me of Richardson's <i>Pamela, or Virtue Rewarded</i>.<br/><br/>-She has nothing which a man could find desirable. One can only see that once upon a time she was utterly beautiful.<br/><br/>-They spread out their star maps and read the heavens like a book. But they are the authors of the book, and the sta</span>
  <span id="freeText12673708143684108890" style="display:none">The first person narration of the story told through constant journal entries became a little forced at times; Lagerkvist gave context and a sense of time to each entry which made it feel artificial. The structure reminds me of Richardson's <i>Pamela, or Virtue Rewarded</i>.<br /><br />-She has nothing which a man could find desirable. One can only see that once upon a time she was utterly beautiful.<br /><br />-They spread out their star maps and read the heavens like a book. But they are the authors of the book, and the stars continue on their shadowy ways and have no inkling of its contents.<br /><br />-What is play? A meaningless dabbling with nothing at all.<br /><br />-Love is something which dies and when dead it rots and becomes soil for a new love. Then the dead love continues its secret life in the living one, and thus in reality there is no death in love.<br /><br />-Don't we own even our faces? Do they belong to anybody who chooses to look at them? And one's body? Can others own one's body? I find the notion most repellent.<br /><br />-The Prince says we need a breathing space and he may be right. Even perpetual successes can be exhausting in the long run. <br /><br />-Some things, though perhaps quite natural in themselves, are really extraordinarily nauseating. <br /><br />-His behavior was so idiotic and tasteless that even I, who have always regarded him as the stupidest of vulgarians, would never have believed him capable of such grotesque buffoonery. <br /><br />-Women always prefer the silliest and most insignificant men, because they remind them of themselves. <br /><br />-I felt like Satan himself, surrounded by all the infernal spirits invoked at their nocturnal meetings, swarming around them with grinning faces, dragging their souls still hot and stinking from their bodies, down into the kingdom of death.<br /><br />-During the fighting and confusion nobody had any time for those who were dying, so they had to expire in their own way as best they could.<br /><br />-People look so strangely idiotic when they are in love, and particularly when they love in vain.<br /><br />-They had fallen asleep like a pair of exhausted children after making their first acquaintance with the bestial instincts of love. <br /><br />-At the same time is was a remarkable experience which filled me with a kind of somber savagery and an awareness of the vanity and ruin of everything.<br /><br />-But she slipped through his hands, imperceptibly and without any explanations. Doubtless that increased his love; it generally seems to. <br /><br />-When believers have the choice between that which is true and that which is not, they always choose that which is not. Lies are far rarer and more impressive than the truth, and so they prefer them. <br /><br />-Human beings like to see themselves reflected in clouded mirrors. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
