<div id="review_567779888" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/567779888" />
    <a title="Jos" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18102778-jos"><img alt="Jos" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365338097p2/18102778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/567779888?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jos" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jos" href="/user/show/18102778-jos">Jos</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/17933833-der-zwerg">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18102778-jos?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18102778-jos?shelf=scandinavian-lit">scandinavian-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer567779888" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10733156070364372276">I read Lagerkvist's Barrabas before. While I really enjoyed his writing, I couldn't connect to the topic of faith and the inability to believe. The Dwarf seemed like the perfect combination of writer and topic. It didn't disappoint, this is a great novel.<br/>The story takes place in a renaissance principality in Italy. The dwarf serves the sovereign in various functions but he insists that he's not the court's jester. The humiliations and rejections he experienced from early childhood onwards made h</span>
  <span id="freeText10733156070364372276" style="display:none">I read Lagerkvist's Barrabas before. While I really enjoyed his writing, I couldn't connect to the topic of faith and the inability to believe. The Dwarf seemed like the perfect combination of writer and topic. It didn't disappoint, this is a great novel.<br />The story takes place in a renaissance principality in Italy. The dwarf serves the sovereign in various functions but he insists that he's not the court's jester. The humiliations and rejections he experienced from early childhood onwards made him bitter and misanthropic. He is contemptuous of any positive human sentiments, be it love, peacefulness or compassion. No pleasure finds his approval, he doesn't laugh, eats scarcely and even is disgusted by any function of the human body. His respect is for the strong, ruthless and brutal. He only enjoys himself in destructive acts against other humans. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[His greatest moments are his participation in a war his prince wages against his neighbour, a consecutive mass poisoning at the feast intended to confirm everlasting peace, the betrayal of the ruler's daughter ending with the death of her lover and his advancement of the princess' religious insanity. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />The dwarf incorporates all evil men do. The message in the context of its time is clear. It's a message against the inhumanity of fascism that was in full swing in Germany and its allied countries. Given Lagerkvist's occupation with humanity and its driving forces, his message extends beyond the specific. The final scene where the dwarf is incarcerated, supports this interpretation. The dwarf takes his sentence as a temporary one. He's certain that he will be needed again. This leaves the reader on a pessimistic note. Evil can be suspended but it will continue to brood beneath the surface to erupt again. Continous efforts are needed to contain the strong forces corrupting mankind and it has to start within the individual. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10733156070364372276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_43176307" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43176307" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/43176307?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=great-books-group">great-books-group</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=nobel-prize-author">nobel-prize-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=scandinavian-lit">scandinavian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=read-and-reread">read-and-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=modern-classic">modern-classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43176307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5409718179611366067">This was my introduction to Lagerkvist and what a book it is. I found it unique in my experience and well worth rereading. It is narrated by the title Dwarf, 26 inches high, at the court of an Italian City-state in the renaissance. He is the narrator of the story, obviously obsessed by writing down his experiences in a form of diary. Everything in the novel is described from his particular viewpoint, mostly in retrospect, ranging from a few hours or minutes to several weeks or months after the a</span>
  <span id="freeText5409718179611366067" style="display:none">This was my introduction to Lagerkvist and what a book it is. I found it unique in my experience and well worth rereading. It is narrated by the title Dwarf, 26 inches high, at the court of an Italian City-state in the renaissance. He is the narrator of the story, obviously obsessed by writing down his experiences in a form of diary. Everything in the novel is described from his particular viewpoint, mostly in retrospect, ranging from a few hours or minutes to several weeks or months after the actual events. The dwarf is a profound misanthrope and generally embodies all things evil. He hates almost every person at the court except for the prince (who is the ruler of the city-state, rather king than prince), or rather aspects of him. He loves war, brutality and fixed positions. While almost all other characters of the novel develop during the chain of events, the dwarf does not change. He is still exactly the same character from the first to the last page. He is deeply religious, but his take on Christianity includes the belief in a non-forgiving God. He is impressed with Bernardo's science but soon repelled by its relentless search for truth.<br />The exact locations are unclear, but since the character named Bernardo, which is unmistakably modeled on Leonardo da Vinci, appears in the novel and it is full of political inrigue that is truly Machiavellian the setting is not in doubt. In addition it is considered his most important novel and the most artistically innovative. If you are interested in Renaissance history or the politics of Machiavelli you are sure to find this novel a great read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5409718179611366067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('y2wNn+970Ft9l0pD6kLHlKbrhlwh8DTlC//sLFTA9KKC1KSr1wV83ZhHHL0tYtJyDam0zCjnSe0RKGSrrdxJGg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HUVfoTOqzZgb5yGD8qNzDFnp1YIkRVw8jmEmGraBa/1U/faVC9RhHv43d301g2bq8qvnEi1SITSUtq6dT53WRQ==')})
  }

Kall has invented a drug, kallocain, which denies the privacy of thought and is the final step towards the transmutation of the individual human being into a &quot;happy, healthy cell in the state organism.&quot; For, says Leo, &quot;from thoughts and feelings, words and actions are born. How then could these thoughts and feelings belong to the individual? Doesn\'t the whole fellow-soldier belong to the state? To whom should his thoughts and feelings belong then, if not to the state?&quot;\nAs the first-person record of Leo Kall, scientist, fellow-soldier too late disillusioned to undo his previous actions, Kallocain achieves a chilling power and veracity that place it among the finest novels to emerge from the strife-torn Europe of the twentieth century.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"144102673494108222\" With sly humor, poor farmhand Johan Johansson (Bragging Johnny to his friends) relates the story of his family and the villainous father and son who own their land. Whenever the Johanssons can\'t pay their rent, the landlords possess themselves of their women; the debt is then temporarily canceled and credit extended. Along with his scripture-citing family and neighbors, Johan struggles to find the hand of Providence in this oppressive, ultimately incestuous arrangement, which ends with a mysterious (though equally arbitrary) &quot;act of God.&quot;\n\nAlthough the characters in this parable are simply drawn, the women in Johnny\'s family make a powerful impression on both narrator and reader. are they that defile the man.&quot;\n\nGeddes\'s translation is remarkable for its sensitivity to the characters\' Bible-tinged speech.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3677396106287464437\" href=\"#\"           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8769227080166854962">Lagerkvist was born in 1891 in southern Sweden. In 1910 he went to Uppsala as a student and in 1913 he left for Paris, where he was exposed to the work of Pablo Picasso. He studied Middle Age Art, as well as Indian and Chinese literature, to prepare himself for becoming a poet. His first collection of poetry was published in 1916. In 1940 Lagerkvist was chosen as one of the "aderton" (the eighteen</span>
  <span id="freeText8769227080166854962" style="display:none">Lagerkvist was born in 1891 in southern Sweden. In 1910 he went to Uppsala as a student and in 1913 he left for Paris, where he was exposed to the work of Pablo Picasso. He studied Middle Age Art, as well as Indian and Chinese literature, to prepare himself for becoming a poet. His first collection of poetry was published in 1916. In 1940 Lagerkvist was chosen as one of the "aderton" (the eighteen) of the Swedish Academy.<br /><br />Lagerkvist wrote poetry, novels, plays, short stories and essays. He was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1951 "for the artistic vigour and true independence of mind with which he endeavours in his poetry to find answers to the eternal questions confronting mankind."</span>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;And they are deformed though it does not show on the outside. I live only my dwarf life. I never go around tall and smooth-featured. I am ever myself, always the same, I live one life alone. I have no other being inside me. And I recognize everything within me, nothing ever comes up from my inner depths, nothing there is shrouded in mystery. Therefore I do not fear the things which frighten them, the incoherent, the unknown, the mysterious. Such things do not exist for me. There is nothing "different" about me.&rdquo;</span>
